> /opt/miniconda3/envs/aider/bin/aider  
> Model: gpt-4  
> Git repo: .git  
> Repo-map: universal-ctags using 1024 tokens  
> Use /help to see in-chat commands, run with --help to see cmd line args  

#### /help  
> /add Add matching files to the chat session using glob patterns  
> /clear Clear the chat history  
> /commit Commit edits to the repo made outside the chat (commit message optional)  
> /diff Display the diff of the last aider commit  
> /drop Remove matching files from the chat session  
> /exit Exit the application  
> /git Run a git command  
> /help Show help about all commands  
> /ls List all known files and those included in the chat session  
> /run Run a shell command and optionally add the output to the chat  
> /tokens Report on the number of tokens used by the current chat context  
> /undo Undo the last git commit if it was done by aider  
> /voice Record and transcribe voice input

#### Can you help me explain the overview of this project and its tech stack?  

Based on the file structure and the file names you've provided, it seems like this project is a web application. Here's a brief overview of the tech stack and the role of each component:

1. **Node.js**: The presence of `.js` files and the `routes` directory suggests that this project is using Node.js, a JavaScript runtime built on Chrome's V8 JavaScript engine. Node.js is used for building fast and scalable network applications.

2. **Express.js**: The `routes` directory also suggests that this project is using Express.js, a minimal and flexible Node.js web application framework that provides a robust set of features for web and mobile applications. It's often used to build APIs.

3. **jQuery**: The `jquery.min.js` and `jquery.dataTables.min.js` files suggest that this project is using jQuery, a fast, small, and feature-rich JavaScript library. It makes things like HTML document traversal and manipulation, event handling, and animation much simpler with an easy-to-use API that works across a multitude of browsers.

4. **Bootstrap**: The `bootstrap.bundle.min.js` file suggests that this project is using Bootstrap, the most popular HTML, CSS, and JS framework for developing responsive, mobile-first projects on the web.

5. **DataTables**: The `jquery.dataTables.min.js` file suggests that this project is using DataTables, a plug-in for the jQuery Javascript library. It's a highly flexible tool, built upon the foundations of progressive enhancement, that adds advanced interaction controls to any HTML table.

6. **Leaflet**: The `leaflet.js` file in the `public/leaflet` directory suggests that this project is using Leaflet, a leading open-source JavaScript library for mobile-friendly interactive maps.

7. **Moment.js**: The `moment.min.js` file suggests that this project is using Moment.js, a JavaScript library that helps creating, manipulating, and formatting dates.

8. **Sentry**: The `sentry.min.js` file suggests that this project is using Sentry, an open-source error tracking tool that helps developers monitor and fix crashes in real time.

9. **Highlight.js**: The `highlight.min.js` file suggests that this project is using Highlight.js, a syntax highlighter written in JavaScript. It works in the browser as well as on the server. It works with pretty much any markup, doesn’t depend on any framework, and has automatic language detection.

10. **Chart.js**: The `chart.min.js` file suggests that this project is using Chart.js, a simple yet flexible JavaScript charting for designers & developers.

Please note that this is a high-level overview based on the file names and directories. For a more detailed understanding, we would need to look into the codebase.

#### Give me an overview of this project and the tech stack that its using  

Based on the file structure and the file names, it appears that this project is a web application that uses a JavaScript-based tech stack. Here's a brief overview of the technologies that seem to be in use:

1. **Node.js**: The presence of files with `.js` extension in the `app` and `routes` directories suggests that the server-side logic is written in Node.js. The `api` and `router` terms in the filenames suggest a RESTful API architecture.

2. **Express.js**: The `router` term in the filenames also suggests the use of Express.js, a popular web application framework for Node.js, for routing.

3. **jQuery**: The `jquery.min.js` and `jquery.dataTables.min.js` files in the `public/js` directory suggest the use of jQuery, a fast, small, and feature-rich JavaScript library.

#### Help me rewrite this codebase to React, lets do this one step at a time. How should we approach this?  

Rewriting a codebase to React is a significant task that requires careful planning and execution. Here's a high-level approach to this task:

1. **Understand the Current Codebase**: Before starting the rewrite, it's crucial to understand the current codebase thoroughly. This includes understanding the functionality of each component, how data flows through the application, and any dependencies that exist.

2. **Plan the New Structure**: React applications are typically structured as a hierarchy of components. Plan out this hierarchy based on the functionality of the current application. Identify which parts of the application will become individual React components.

3. **Set Up the React Environment**: Set up a new React project using Create React App or a similar tool. This will provide a base structure for the new codebase.

4. **Rewrite Components One at a Time**: Start rewriting the application one component at a time. This approach allows you to test each component individually and ensure that it works correctly before moving on to the next one.

5. **Maintain State Management**: Depending on the complexity of your application, you might need to use a state management library like Redux or Context API. This will help you manage the state of your application more efficiently.

6. **Test Each Component**: As you rewrite each component, make sure to write tests for it. This will help ensure that the new React codebase works as expected and matches the functionality of the original codebase.

7. **Integrate Components**: Once all components are rewritten and tested, integrate them together. Test the application as a whole to ensure everything works together correctly.

8. **Optimize and Refactor**: After the initial rewrite is complete, go through the codebase and look for opportunities to optimize and refactor. This could include using hooks, splitting large components into smaller ones, and improving performance.

Please note that this is a high-level approach and the specific steps may vary based on the complexity and structure of your current codebase. 

Since you've shared the `app.js` file, we can start by identifying the main components of your application and planning out the new React structure. Could you please share the content of the `app.js` file?

#### We are going to refactor this codebase to React, what should be the overall approach to do this  

Refactoring a codebase to React involves several steps. Here's a high-level approach:

1. **Understand the Current Codebase**: Before you start refactoring, it's crucial to understand the current codebase. This includes understanding the architecture, the libraries used, the data flow, etc.

2. **Plan the Refactoring Process**: Refactoring a codebase is a significant task, and it's essential to plan it properly. Identify the parts of the application that can be easily refactored to React and start with them. Also, identify the parts that will require more effort and plan them accordingly.

3. **Set Up a React Environment**: You'll need to set up a React environment in your project. This includes installing React and other necessary libraries like Babel and Webpack.

4. **Identify Components**: React is all about components. Identify the components in your application. A component could be a button, a form, a header, etc.

5. **Start Refactoring**: Start refactoring your application one component at a time. It's usually best to start with the leaf components (components that don't have any children) and then move up the component hierarchy.

6. **State Management**: Decide on how you want to manage the state in your application. You can use React's local state or use libraries like Redux or MobX for global state management.

7. **Routing**: If your application has multiple pages, you'll need to set up routing. You can use libraries like React Router for this.

8. **Testing**: Make sure to write tests for your React components. You can use libraries like Jest and Enzyme for this.

9. **Optimization**: Once you have refactored your application, you can look into optimizing it. This could include code splitting, lazy loading, etc.

10. **Deployment**: Finally, you'll need to deploy your refactored application. This will involve building your React application and deploying it to a server.

Remember, refactoring a codebase is a significant task and should be done incrementally. It's also important to keep the rest of your team informed about the changes you're making.


#### Lets start with the first step, understanding the codebase  

Absolutely, understanding the existing codebase is the first and crucial step in the refactoring process. Here's how you can approach it:

1. **Identify the Technologies Used**: Look at the package.json file (if it's a JavaScript project) or other configuration files to understand what libraries and frameworks are currently being used.

2. **Understand the Architecture**: Look at how the project is structured. Identify the main components of the application and how they interact with each other. This includes understanding the data flow, state management, routing, etc.

3. **Understand the Functionality**: Run the application and play around with it to understand what it does. This will give you a good idea of the user interface and the user experience.

4. **Read the Code**: Go through the codebase file by file, function by function. Try to understand what each part of the code does. This can be a time-consuming process, but it's essential for a successful refactoring.

5. **Document Your Findings**: As you're going through the codebase, document your findings. This could include diagrams of the architecture, notes on the functionality of different parts of the code, etc. This documentation will be very useful during the refactoring process.

6. **Identify Potential Challenges**: As you're understanding the codebase, identify parts that could be challenging to refactor. This could be because of complex logic, tight coupling, etc. Make a note of these challenges as they will need to be addressed during the refactoring process.

7. **Ask Questions**: If you're part of a team, don't hesitate to ask questions. Your teammates might have valuable insights and knowledge about the codebase.

Remember, the goal of this step is to understand the codebase as thoroughly as possible before starting the refactoring process. The better you understand the existing codebase, the smoother the refactoring process will be.

#### We are going to rewrite this codebase to use React and Blockchain.com as an API for the service. List out the necessary steps to start this refactoring and what you would need me to add.  

To refactor the codebase to use React and Blockchain.com as an API service, we would need to follow these steps:

1. **Identify the components**: We need to identify the components in the current codebase that can be converted into React components. This includes both UI components (like buttons, forms, etc.) and logical components (like API calls, data processing, etc.).

2. **Create the React project**: We need to create a new React project where we will write our new code. This can be done using `create-react-app` or any other preferred method.

3. **Rewrite the components**: Once we have identified the components, we need to rewrite them in React. This includes converting the HTML to JSX, and rewriting the JavaScript to use React's state and props system.

4. **Integrate the Blockchain.com API**: We need to replace any existing API calls with calls to the Blockchain.com API. This will likely involve rewriting some of the logic components to handle the new API.

5. **Test the new codebase**: After all the components have been rewritten and the new API integrated, we need to thoroughly test the new codebase to ensure everything works as expected.

To start this process, I would need you to add the following files to the chat:

- Any HTML files that contain the UI for the application.
- Any JavaScript files that contain the logic for the application.
- Any CSS files that contain the styles for the application.
- Any other files that are relevant to the functionality of the application.

Once you have added these files, I can start identifying the components and planning the refactoring process.

#### Using these files lets refactor this code using React  

Refactoring this codebase to use React would be a significant undertaking, as it would involve rewriting the entire frontend of the application. React is a JavaScript library for building user interfaces, and it would replace the current Pug templates used in this project.

Before we start, we need to make sure we have all the necessary files. Currently, we only have access to `package.json` and `README.md`. We would need access to all the `.pug` files in the `views` directory, as well as the JavaScript files in the `public/js` directory, as these would be the main files we would need to modify.

Please add these files to the chat so we can proceed with the refactoring.

Alright, let's start by creating a new React application. We'll need to install `create-react-app` globally if it's not already installed. You can do this by running `npm install -g create-react-app` in your terminal.

Once `create-react-app` is installed, you can create a new React application by running `npx create-react-app btc-rpc-explorer-react`.

This will create a new directory called `btc-rpc-explorer-react` with a basic React application inside. You can start the application by navigating into the new directory and running `npm start`.

```javascript
      {/* Here we'll add the common layout elements like header, footer, etc. */}
      {children}
    </div>
  );
};

export default Layout;
>>>>>>> updated
```

In both files, the components are exported as default exports, and they are imported correctly in `App.js`. This should work as expected.

If you're still seeing the error, it's possible that the issue is in another part of your application. Please check all your other components to make sure they are correctly exported and imported. If you're using named exports, make sure you're using curly braces `{}` in your import statements. If you're using default exports, there should be no curly braces in the import statements.

> Repo-map can't include /Users/gilbertoarellano/Dropbox/Academia/Chapman/Fall 2023/CPSC 370/CPSC-370_BTC_derivates_AI_Chapman/workspaces/btc-rpc-explorer/btc-rpc-explorer-react/components/Layout.js  
> Repo-map can't include /Users/gilbertoarellano/Dropbox/Academia/Chapman/Fall 2023/CPSC 370/CPSC-370_BTC_derivates_AI_Chapman/workspaces/btc-rpc-explorer/btc-rpc-explorer-react/utils.js  

To recreate the application bitcoinexplorer.org using React, we will need to create a new React application and set up the basic structure. Here are the steps we need to follow:

1. Create a new React application using Create React App (CRA).
2. Set up the basic structure of the application, including the main App component and other necessary components.
3. Fetch the Bitcoin data from an API and store it in the state of the App component.
4. Pass the Bitcoin data to the necessary components and display it in the UI.

Let's start with the first step, creating a new React application. You can do this by running the following command in your terminal:

```bash
npx create-react-app bitcoin-explorer
```

Once the new React application is created, we can start setting up the basic structure of the application. We will need the following components:

- App: This will be the main component of the application. It will fetch the Bitcoin data from the API and store it in its state. It will also render the other components and pass the necessary data to them.
- BitcoinData: This component will display the basic Bitcoin data, such as amounts, transaction inputs and outputs, block height, and miners.

Here is the basic structure of these components:

```jsx
// src/App.js
import React from 'react';
import BitcoinData from './BitcoinData';

class App extends React.Component {
  state = {
    bitcoinData: null,
  };

  componentDidMount() {
    // Fetch the Bitcoin data from the API and store it in the state
  }

  render() {
    return (
      <div>
        <h1>Bitcoin Explorer</h1>
        {this.state.bitcoinData && <BitcoinData data={this.state.bitcoinData} />}
      </div>
    );
  }
}

export default App;
```

```jsx
// src/BitcoinData.js
import React from 'react';

const BitcoinData = ({ data }) => (
  <div>
    {/* Display the Bitcoin data */}
  </div>
);

export default BitcoinData;
```

In the next step, we will fetch the Bitcoin data from the API and store it in the state of the App component. We will use the fetch API to do this. The exact API endpoint will depend on the API you are using to get the Bitcoin data.

Finally, we will pass the Bitcoin data to the BitcoinData component and display it in the UI. The exact way to do this will depend on the structure of the Bitcoin data.

Please note that this is a very basic structure of the application. Depending on the exact requirements, you might need to add more components, use a state management library like Redux, add routing with React Router, etc.

Here's how you can create the files and add the code I provided:

1. Open your terminal.
2. Navigate to the directory where you want to create the new React application.
3. Run the following command to create a new React application:

```bash
npx create-react-app bitcoin-explorer
```

4. Once the command completes, navigate into the new directory:

```bash
cd bitcoin-explorer
```

5. Create a new file in the `src` directory named `BitcoinData.js`:

```bash
touch src/BitcoinData.js
```

6. Open the `src/BitcoinData.js` file in your text editor and add the following code:

```jsx

import React from 'react';

  <div>
    {/* Display the Bitcoin data */}
  </div>
);

export default BitcoinData;
```

7. Open the `src/App.js` file in your text editor and replace the existing code with the following code:

```jsx
// src/App.js
import React from 'react';
import BitcoinData from './BitcoinData';

class App extends React.Component {
  state = {
    bitcoinData: null,
  };

  componentDidMount() {
    // Fetch the Bitcoin data from the API and store it in the state
  }

  render() {
    return (
      <div>
        <h1>Bitcoin Explorer</h1>
        {this.state.bitcoinData && <BitcoinData data={this.state.bitcoinData} />}
      </div>
    );
  }
}

export default App;
```

8. Save the changes and close the text editor.
9. Back in the terminal, start the React development server:

```bash
npm start
```

Your new React application should now be running at `http://localhost:3000`.

Next, we need to modify the `LatestBlocks.js` file to fetch data from the blockchain.com API and display it. 

Here's how we can do it:

1. Import the `axios` library at the top of the file.
2. Create a `useEffect` hook to fetch data from the API when the component mounts.
3. Store the fetched data in a state variable using the `useState` hook.
4. Map over the state variable to display the data.

#### do you need to adjust anything in the `btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/App.js`?  

Yes, there is a redundancy in the `btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/App.js` file. The `App` component is fetching data from the blockchain.com API, but it's not using the data. The `LatestBlocks` component is also fetching data from the same API. 

We should remove the API call from the `App` component because it's not necessary. The `LatestBlocks` component should be responsible for fetching its own data.

Here is the *edit block* for the necessary change:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/App.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
import React, { useState, useEffect } from 'react';
import LatestBlocks from './components/LatestBlocks';
import './App.css';

function App() {
  const [blocks, setBlocks] = useState([]);

  useEffect(() => {
    const fetchData = async () => {
      try {
        const response = await fetch('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json');
        const data = await response.json();
        //setBlocks(data.blocks); // Adjust this line based on the API's response structure
      } catch (error) {
        console.error('Error fetching blocks:', error);
      }
    };

    fetchData();
  }, []);

  return (
    <div className="App">
      <LatestBlocks blocks={blocks} />
    </div>
  );
}

export default App;
=======
import React from 'react';
import LatestBlocks from './components/LatestBlocks';
import './App.css';

function App() {
  return (
    <div className="App">
      <LatestBlocks />
    </div>
  );
}

export default App;
```

#### AxiosError Prototype  

The error message indicates that the request to the API endpoint 'https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json' failed with a 404 status code, which means the requested resource could not be found on the server.

This could be due to a number of reasons:

1. The API endpoint is incorrect.
2. The API endpoint has been changed or removed.
3. The server is temporarily down or experiencing issues.

To resolve this issue, you should:

1. Verify the API endpoint: Check the blockchain.com API documentation to ensure that the endpoint 'https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json' is correct. If it's not, update the endpoint in the `LatestBlocks.js` file.

2. Check the server status: If the API endpoint is correct, the server might be down or experiencing issues. You can check the status of the blockchain.com API on their status page or by contacting their support.

3. Handle the error: Regardless of the cause of the error, your code should handle it gracefully. This could involve displaying an error message to the user, retrying the request, or providing a fallback.

If the API endpoint needs to be updated, please provide the correct endpoint. If the server is down or experiencing issues, you might need to wait until it's back up and running. If you want to improve the error handling, I can help you implement that.

#### The tx hash is in reverse byte order. What this means is that in order to get the html transaction hash from the JSON tx hash for the following transaction, you need to decode the hex. This will produce a binary output, which you need to reverse (the last 8bits/1byte move to the front, second to last 8bits/1byte needs to be moved to second, etc.). Then once the reversed bytes are decoded, you will get the html transaction hash.

The error you're encountering is a CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) issue. This is a security feature implemented by web browsers to prevent requests to different domains, protocols, or ports than the one from which the current document originated.

In your case, you're trying to make a request from your local development server (http://localhost:3000) to the blockchain.com API. The browser is blocking this request because the server at blockchain.com is not set up to accept requests from your origin (http://localhost:3000).

To solve this issue, you can use a proxy server to make the request on behalf of your application. This can be done by adding a `proxy` field to your `package.json` file in your React application.

Here's how you can do it:

```json
btc-rpc-explorer-react/package.json
<<<<<<< HEAD
{
  "name": "btc-rpc-explorer-react",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "description": "",
  "main": "index.js",
  "scripts": {
    "start": "react-scripts start",
    "build": "react-scripts build",
    "test": "react-scripts test",
    "eject": "react-scripts eject"
  },
  "keywords": [],
  "author": "",
  "license": "ISC",
  "dependencies": {
    "axios": "^0.21.1",
    "react": "^17.0.2",
    "react-dom": "^17.0.2",
    "react-scripts": "4.0.3"
  }
}
=======
{
  "name": "btc-rpc-explorer-react",
  "version": "1.0.0",
  "description": "",
  "main": "index.js",
  "scripts": {
    "start": "react-scripts start",
    "build": "react-scripts build",
    "test": "react-scripts test",
    "eject": "react-scripts eject"
  },
  "keywords": [],
  "author": "",
  "license": "ISC",
  "dependencies": {
    "axios": "^0.21.1",
    "react": "^17.0.2",
    "react-dom": "^17.0.2",
    "react-scripts": "4.0.3"
  },
  "proxy": "https://blockchain.info"
}
>>>>>>> updated
```

This tells the development server to proxy any unknown requests (requests that did not match a static file) to https://blockchain.info.

The error message indicates that `blocks` is `undefined` at the time when the component is trying to map over it. This is likely because the initial state for `blocks` is set to an empty array and then it's updated asynchronously when the API call returns. If the API call hasn't completed by the time the component tries to render, `blocks` will be `undefined`.

To fix this, we need to ensure that `blocks` is always an array. We can do this by checking if `blocks` is `undefined` before trying to map over it. If it is `undefined`, we can default to an empty array.

Here is the change you need to make in the `LatestBlocks.js` file:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
      {blocks.map((block) => (
=======
      {(blocks || []).map((block) => (
>>>>>>> updated
        <div className="block-row" key={block.height}>
          <div className="block-col">{block.height}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{new Date(block.time * 1000).toLocaleString()}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.n_tx}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.size}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.mrkl_root}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.nonce}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.hash}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.prev_block}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.fee}</div>
          <div className="block-col">{block.main_chain ? 'Yes' : 'No'}</div>
        </div>
      ))}
```

This change will ensure that `blocks` is always an array when we try to map over it, preventing the error you're seeing.

> btc-rpc-explorer-react/public/manifest.json  
> Add these files to the chat? n  

From the console log, it seems like the response from the `/latestblock` endpoint is an object with properties `block_index`, `hash`, `height`, `time`, and `txIndexes`. However, in your `LatestBlocks.js` file, you're trying to map over `blocks` as if it's an array.

This discrepancy is likely why the data isn't being displayed. You're trying to map over an object, not an array.

To fix this, you need to adjust how you're handling the response from the `/latestblock` endpoint. Instead of setting `blocks` to `response.data.blocks`, you should set `blocks` to an array that contains `response.data`.

Here's the updated code for `LatestBlocks.js`:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
    axios.get('/latestblock')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data.blocks);
      })
=======
    axios.get('/latestblock')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks([response.data]);
      })
>>>>>>> updated
```

This change will ensure that `blocks` is always an array when mapping over it, even when the `/latestblock` endpoint returns an object. Let me know if you have any further issues or if there's anything else I can assist you with.

> Commit c4d9642 aider: Updated how `blocks` is set in `LatestBlocks.js` to ensure it is always an array when mapping over it.

#### Perfect, can you aling these rows with the header, and its only producing one row of data, can you do 5 rows  

To align the rows with the header, we need to modify the CSS file that is associated with the `LatestBlocks.js` component. However, you haven't provided the CSS file. Could you please add the `LatestBlocks.css` file to the chat?

Regarding the second part of your request, to display 5 rows of data instead of one, we need to modify the `LatestBlocks.js` file. Currently, the code is set to display only the latest block. We need to modify the API endpoint to fetch the latest 5 blocks and update the state accordingly.

Here is the change for the `LatestBlocks.js` file:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
    axios.get('/latestblock')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks([response.data]);
      })
=======
    axios.get('/latestblocks?count=5')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data);
      })
>>>>>>> updated
```

Please note that this change assumes that your API supports the `count` parameter in the `latestblocks` endpoint and returns the specified number of latest blocks. If your API doesn't support this, you will need to modify your API accordingly.

The error message indicates that the server is responding with a 404 status code, which means the requested resource could not be found on the server. This could be due to an incorrect URL or the resource does not exist.

In your case, the axios request is trying to get data from '/latestblocks?count=5'. This might not be the correct endpoint or the server might not be set up to handle this request.

To resolve this issue, you need to:

1. Verify the URL you are using to make the axios request. Make sure the endpoint '/latestblocks?count=5' exists on the server and is set up to handle GET requests.
2. If the URL is correct, check the server-side code to see if there is an issue with how the server handles this endpoint.
3. If you are running the server locally, make sure it is running and accessible when you make the axios request.

Since the error is related to the axios request in the `LatestBlocks.js` file, we need to modify this file. #### Unspent Outputs  
#### https://blockchain.info/unspent?active=$address  
#### Multiple Addresses Allowed separated by "|"  
#### Address can be base58 or xpub  
#### Optional limit parameter to show n transactions e.g. &limit=50 (Default: 250, Max: 1000)  
#### Optional confirmations parameter to limit the minimum confirmations e.g. &confirmations=6  
#### The tx hash is in reverse byte order. What this means is that in order to get the html transaction hash from the JSON tx hash for the following transaction, you need to decode the hex. This will produce a binary output, which you need to reverse (the last 8bits/1byte move to the front, second to last 8bits/1byte needs to be moved to second, etc.). Then once the reversed bytes are decoded, you will get the html transaction hash.  
#### {  
####   "unspent_outputs": [  
####     {  
####       "tx_age": "1322659106",  
####       "tx_hash": "e6452a2cb71aa864aaa959e647e7a4726a22e640560f199f79b56b5502114c37",  
####       "tx_index": "12790219",  
####       "tx_output_n": "0",  
####       "script": "76a914641ad5051edd97029a003fe9efb29359fcee409d88ac",  
####       "value": "5000661330"  
####     }  
####   ]  
#### }  
#### Balance  
#### https://blockchain.info/balance?active=$address  
#### Multiple Addresses Allowed separated by "|"  
#### Address can be base58 or xpub  
#### List the balance summary of each address listed.  
#### {  
####   "1MDUoxL1bGvMxhuoDYx6i11ePytECAk9QK": {  
####     "final_balance": 0,

#### [Error] Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:  
#### AxiosError  
####   
#### code: "ERR_NETWORK"  
####   
#### config: {transitional: {silentJSONParsing: true, forcedJSONParsing: true, clarifyTimeoutError: false}, adapter: ["xhr", "http"], transformRequest: Array, transformResponse: Array, timeout: 0, …}  
####   
#### message: "Network Error"  
####   
#### name: "AxiosError"  
####   
#### request: XMLHttpRequest {listeners: Object, onreadystatechange: null, readyState: 4, timeout: 0, withCredentials: false, …}  
####   
#### stack: "AxiosError@http://localhost:3000/static/js/bundle.js:42320:27↵handleError@http://localhost:3000/static/js/bundle.js:41707:77"  
####   
#### AxiosError Prototype  
#### 	(anonymous function) (bundle.js:124)  
#### [Error] Failed to load resource: Origin http://localhost:3000 is not allowed by Access-Control-Allow-Origin. Status code: 200 (latestblock, line 0)  

The error you're encountering is a CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) issue. This is a security feature implemented by browsers to prevent requests to different domains unless the server allows it. 

In this case, the server at `https://blockchain.info/latestblock` is not allowing your request from `http://localhost:3000`. 

To solve this issue, you can use a proxy server to make the request on behalf of your application. This can be done by creating a server-side script that makes the request to the blockchain API and returns the response to your client-side application.

Here are the steps to solve this issue:

1. Create a new file in the `src` directory named `proxy.js`. This file will contain the server-side script that makes the request to the blockchain API.

2. Modify the `LatestBlocks.js` file to make the request to the new `proxy.js` script instead of directly to the blockchain API.

Here are the changes you need to make:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
=======
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/latestblock')
    .then(response => {
      res.json(response.data);
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
    });
};
>>>>>>> updated
```

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
    axios.get('https://blockchain.info/latestblock')
=======
    axios.get('/proxy')
>>>>>>> updated
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data);
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
```

Please note that this solution requires a server-side environment to run the `proxy.js` script. If you're running this application in a purely client-side environment (like a static website), you might need to use a third-party service to bypass the CORS issue.

The error message indicates that the request to `https://www.blockchain.com/proxy` is being blocked by the browser due to CORS policy. This is because the server at `https://www.blockchain.com` does not allow requests from `http://localhost:3000`.

To solve this issue, you need to have a server running that can handle the `/proxy` route and forward the request to the blockchain API. This server should be running on the same domain as your application (in this case, `http://localhost:3000`) to avoid CORS issues.

The `proxy.js` file you have created is a good start, but it needs to be part of a running server. You can use Express.js to create a simple server that uses this `proxy.js` script.

Here are the steps to solve this issue:

1. Install Express.js in your project by running `npm install express` in your project directory.
2. Create a new file named `server.js` in the root directory of your project.
3. In the `server.js` file, import Express.js and your `proxy.js` script, create an Express.js app, and add a route that uses the `proxy.js` script.
4. Start the server by running `node server.js` in your project directory.

Here are the code changes you need to make:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/server.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
=======
const express = require('express');
const proxy = require('./src/proxy');

const app = express();

app.use('/proxy', proxy);

app.listen(3000, () => {
  console.log('Server is running on http://localhost:3000');
});
>>>>>>> updated
```

Please note that this will start the server on port 3000, which is the same port your React app is running on. You may need to change the port number for either the server or the React app to avoid conflicts. If you change the port number for the server, make sure to update the URL in the `LatestBlocks.js` file accordingly.

#### I am getting this error after running node server.js: Cannot GET /  

The error message "Cannot GET /" typically means that the server doesn't have a route handler for the root URL ("/").

In your `server.js` file, you have defined a route handler for "/proxy", but not for "/". If you want to serve a static file or a message when the root URL is accessed, you can add a route handler for it.

Here is an example of how you can add a route handler for the root URL that sends a simple message:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/server.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
const express = require('express');
const proxy = require('./src/proxy');

const app = express();

app.use('/proxy', proxy);

app.listen(3000, () => {
  console.log('Server is running on http://localhost:3000');
});
=======
const express = require('express');
const proxy = require('./src/proxy');

const app = express();

app.use('/proxy', proxy);

app.get('/', (req, res) => {
  res.send('Server is running. Use /proxy for the proxy service.');
});

app.listen(3000, () => {
  console.log('Server is running on http://localhost:3000');
});
>>>>>>> updated
```

This *edit block* adds a new route handler for the root URL ("/") that sends a simple message when accessed. After this change, if you navigate to http://localhost:3000 in your web browser, you should see the message "Server is running. Use /proxy for the proxy service." instead of the "Cannot GET /" error.

#### Now what do I do, I am getting this on the localhost: Server is running. Use /proxy for the proxy service.  

The message "Server is running. Use /proxy for the proxy service." is the response from the root URL ("/") of your server. This is expected because we added a route handler for the root URL to send this message.

Your React application is trying to fetch data from the "/proxy" endpoint of your server. To see the data, you should open the React application in your web browser. If your React application is running on port 3000, you can access it at http://localhost:3000.

If you want to see the data returned by the "/proxy" endpoint directly, you can navigate to http://localhost:3000/proxy in your web browser. This should return the latest block data from the blockchain.com API.

#### still not working, I was able to retrieve the data before without going through the proxy route. There may be some other way to retrieve the blockchain.com api data?  

Yes, you can directly call the blockchain.com API from your React component without going through the proxy route. However, this might lead to CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) issues, as browsers restrict web applications from making requests to a different domain than the one the web application came from, for security reasons.

To avoid potential CORS issues, it's common to set up a proxy server (like the one you have in your `server.js` file) that makes the API request on behalf of the React application.

If you still want to make the API request directly from the React component, you can modify the `useEffect` hook in your `LatestBlocks.js` file to call the blockchain.com API directly. Here is the *edit block* for this change:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('http://localhost:3000/proxy')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data);
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
  }, []);
=======
useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('https://blockchain.info/latestblock')
      .then(response => {
        console.log(response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data);
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
  }, []);
>>>>>>> updated
```

This *edit block* changes the URL in the `axios.get` call from 'http://localhost:3000/proxy' to 'https://blockchain.info/latestblock', which is the URL of the blockchain.com API. After making this change, your React application will make the API request directly to blockchain.com, instead of going through the proxy route.

The error message `EADDRINUSE` means that the port number which the node.js server wants to bind to is already in use. In your case, it's port 5000. This could be because another instance of the server is already running or another application is using that port.

You have two options to solve this issue:

1. You can stop the other application that is using port 5000. If you're not sure which application this is, on a Unix-based system like Linux or Mac OS, you can use the command `lsof -i :5000` in your terminal to find out. On Windows, you can use the command `netstat -ano | findstr :5000`.

2. You can change the port number that your node.js server binds to. This would involve changing the code in your `server.js` file.

If you want to go with the second option, I can guide you through the changes. Let me know if you want to proceed with this.

The error message indicates that the request to `https://www.blockchain.com/proxy` is being blocked due to CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) policy. This is a security feature that prevents requests to a server from a different origin (domain, protocol, or port) than the one the web page came from.

In your case, the React application is running on `http://localhost:3000` and is trying to make a request to `https://www.blockchain.com/proxy`, which is a different origin.

However, it seems like there's a misunderstanding in the request URL. The request should be made to your local server (which is running on `http://localhost:5001`), not directly to `https://www.blockchain.com/proxy`.

The local server (Node.js server) is supposed to act as a proxy to make requests to `https://blockchain.info/latestblock` (as defined in `btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js`), not `https://www.blockchain.com/proxy`.

So, the request in `btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js` should be updated to point to your local server.

Here is the *edit block* for the change:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
axios.get('http://localhost:3000/proxy')
=======
axios.get('http://localhost:5001/proxy')
>>>>>>> updated
```

This change will make your React application send requests to your local server, which will then proxy the requests to the Blockchain API. After applying this change, you should no longer see the CORS error.

The error message indicates that the server at `http://localhost:5001/proxy` is not allowing cross-origin requests from `http://localhost:3000`. This is a CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) issue.

To fix this, we need to modify the server code to allow CORS from `http://localhost:3000`. We can use the `cors` middleware in Express.js to do this.

The file we need to modify is `btc-rpc-explorer-react/server.js`.

Here are the changes we need to make:

1. Import the `cors` module at the top of the file.
2. Use the `cors` middleware in the Express app, before the routes.

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/server.js

=======

const cors = require('cors');
const proxy = require('./src/proxy');

const app = express();

app.use(cors());
>>>>>>> updated
```

From the error message, it seems like the data returned from the API is not an array but an object. The `map` function is being called on `blocks` which is expected to be an array, but it's actually an object. This is why you're seeing the error.

To fix this, we need to modify the `LatestBlocks.js` file to handle the case where the data returned from the API is an object. We can do this by checking if `blocks` is an object and if it is, we convert it into an array.

Here's how we can do this:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
  useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('http://localhost:5001/proxy')
      .then(response => {
        console.log('Response data:', response.data);
        setBlocks(response.data);
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
  }, []);

  if (!Array.isArray(blocks)) {
    console.error('Unexpected data:', blocks);
    return null;
  }
=======
  useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('http://localhost:5001/proxy')
      .then(response => {
        console.log('Response data:', response.data);
        if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          setBlocks(response.data);
        } else if (typeof response.data === 'object') {
          setBlocks([response.data]);
        }
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
  }, []);

  if (!Array.isArray(blocks)) {
    console.error('Unexpected data:', blocks);
    return null;
  }
>>>>>>> updated
```

This change will ensure that `blocks` is always an array, even when the API returns an object.

The error message indicates that the server is returning a 500 Internal Server Error when trying to fetch data from the blockchain.com API. This error is usually caused by an issue on the server side, not the client side.

The error is being logged in the `LatestBlocks.js` file, which is where the API request is being made. However, the actual issue seems to be in the `src/proxy.js` file, which is where the server is handling the API request and response.

The `src/proxy.js` file is currently checking if the response data from the API is an array, and if it's not, it's wrapping the data in an array before sending the response. However, if there's an error with the API request, the response data might not be an array or an object that can be wrapped in an array, which could be causing the server to throw a 500 error.

To fix this, we need to add error handling in the `src/proxy.js` file to check if the API request was successful before trying to manipulate the response data. If the API request was not successful, we should send an error response instead of trying to wrap the response data in an array.

Here is the *edit block* for the `src/proxy.js` file:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
    .then(response => {
      if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
        res.json(response.data);
      } else {
        res.json([response.data]);
      }
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
    });
};
=======
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
    .then(response => {
      if (response.status === 200) {
        if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          res.json(response.data);
        } else {
          res.json([response.data]);
        }
      } else {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', response.status);
        res.status(response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
    });
};
>>>>>>> updated
```

This *edit block* adds a check for `response.status === 200` to ensure the API request was successful before trying to manipulate the response data. If the API request was not successful, it logs the response status and sends an error response with the same status.

The error message indicates that the server is responding with a 500 Internal Server Error when trying to fetch data from the blockchain.com API. This error is being logged in the `LatestBlocks.js` file, but the actual issue seems to be in the `src/proxy.js` file.

The `src/proxy.js` file is currently checking if the response data from the API is an array and wrapping it in an array if it's not. However, if there's an error with the API request, the response data might not be an array or an object that can be wrapped in an array, causing the server to throw a 500 error.

To fix this, we need to add error handling in the `src/proxy.js` file to check if the API request was successful before manipulating the response data. If the API request was not successful, we should send an error response instead of trying to wrap the response data in an array.

Here is the *edit block* for the `src/proxy.js` file:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
    .then(response => {
      if (response.status === 200) {
        if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          res.json(response.data);
        } else {
          res.json([response.data]);
        }
      } else {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', response.status);
        res.status(response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
    });
};
=======
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
    .then(response => {
      if (response.status === 200) {
        if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          res.json(response.data);
        } else {
          res.json([response.data]);
        }
      } else {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', response.status);
        res.status(response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      if (error.response) {
        res.status(error.response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      } else {
        res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    });
};
>>>>>>> updated
```

This change adds a check in the catch block to see if the error object has a `response` property. If it does, it sends an error response with the status code from the error response. If it doesn't, it sends a 500 error response. This ensures that the server doesn't throw a 500 error when the API request fails.

It seems like the error is coming from the server side when trying to fetch data from the blockchain.com API. The server is returning a 500 Internal Server Error, which means something went wrong on the server side while processing the request.

The error handling code in `src/proxy.js` is designed to handle such situations and send an appropriate error response. However, it seems like the error is not being handled properly in the client-side code in `src/components/LatestBlocks.js`.

Here's what we can do to improve the error handling in the client-side code:

1. Modify the `src/components/LatestBlocks.js` file to handle the error response from the server and display an appropriate error message to the user.

Here's the step-by-step changes:

1. In the `useEffect` hook where we make the API request, we need to check if the response contains an error. If it does, we should set an error state variable with the error message.

2. We need to create a new state variable `error` to store the error message.

3. In the JSX returned by the component, we need to check if the `error` state variable is set. If it is, we should display the error message to the user.

Here's the *edit block* for these changes:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/components/LatestBlocks.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
import React, { useEffect, useState } from 'react';
import axios from 'axios';
import './LatestBlocks.css';

const LatestBlocks = () => {
  const [blocks, setBlocks] = useState([]);

  useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('http://localhost:5001/proxy/blocks')
      .then(response => {
        console.log('Response data:', response.data);
        if (response.data && Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          setBlocks(response.data);
        } else {
          console.error('Unexpected data:', response.data);
        }
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      });
  }, []);

  if (!Array.isArray(blocks)) {
    console.error('Unexpected data:', blocks);
    return null;
  }

  return (
    <div className="latest-blocks">
      <h2>Latest Blocks</h2>
      <div className="block-table">
        <div className="block-header">
          <span>INDEX</span>
          <span>HASH</span>
          <span>HEIGHT</span>
          <span>TIME</span>
          <span>MINER</span>
          <span>TX COUNT</span>
          <span>% FULL</span>
        </div>
        {blocks.map((block) => (
          <div className="block-row" key={block.height}>
            <div className="block-col index">{block.block_index}</div>
            <div className="block-col hash" title={block.hash}>{block.hash.substring(0, 10)}...</div>
            <div className="block-col height">{block.height}</div>
            <div className="block-col time">{new Date(block.time * 1000).toLocaleString()}</div>
            <div className="block-col miner">{block.n_tx}</div>
            <div className="block-col tx-count">{block.txIndexes.length}</div>
            <div className="block-col full">{block.main_chain ? 'Yes' : 'No'}</div>
          </div>
        ))}
      </div>
    </div>
  );
}

export default LatestBlocks;
=======
import React, { useEffect, useState } from 'react';
import axios from 'axios';
import './LatestBlocks.css';

const LatestBlocks = () => {
  const [blocks, setBlocks] = useState([]);
  const [error, setError] = useState(null);

  useEffect(() => {
    axios.get('http://localhost:5001/proxy/blocks')
      .then(response => {
        if (response.data.error) {
          setError(response.data.error);
        } else if (response.data && Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          setBlocks(response.data);
        } else {
          console.error('Unexpected data:', response.data);
        }
      })
      .catch(error => {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
        setError('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API');
      });
  }, []);

  if (error) {
    return <div>Error: {error}</div>;
  }

  if (!Array.isArray(blocks)) {
    console.error('Unexpected data:', blocks);
    return null;
  }

  return (
    <div className="latest-blocks">
      <h2>Latest Blocks</h2>
      <div className="block-table">
        <div className="block-header">
          <span>INDEX</span>
          <span>HASH</span>
          <span>HEIGHT</span>
          <span>TIME</span>
          <span>MINER</span>
          <span>TX COUNT</span>
          <span>% FULL</span>
        </div>
        {blocks.map((block) => (
          <div className="block-row" key={block.height}>
            <div className="block-col index">{block.block_index}</div>
            <div className="block-col hash" title={block.hash}>{block.hash.substring(0, 10)}...</div>
            <div className="block-col height">{block.height}</div>
            <div className="block-col time">{new Date(block.time * 1000).toLocaleString()}</div>
            <div className="block-col miner">{block.n_tx}</div>
            <div className="block-col tx-count">{block.txIndexes.length}</div>
            <div className="block-col full">{block.main_chain ? 'Yes' : 'No'}</div>
          </div>
        ))}
      </div>
    </div>
  );
}

export default LatestBlocks;
>>>>>>> updated
```

#### The tx hash is in reverse byte order. What this means is that in order to get the html transaction hash from the JSON tx hash for the following transaction, you need to decode the hex. This will produce a binary output, which you need to reverse (the last 8bits/1byte move to the front, second to last 8bits/1byte needs to be moved to second, etc.). Then once the reversed bytes are decoded, you will get the html transaction hash.

The error message indicates that the request to the `http://localhost:5001/proxy/blocks` endpoint is returning a 404 status code, which means the requested resource could not be found on the server.

This could be due to several reasons:

1. The server at `http://localhost:5001` is not running.
2. The `/proxy/blocks` endpoint does not exist on the server.
3. There's a network issue preventing the request from reaching the server.

Here are some steps you can take to troubleshoot this issue:

1. Ensure that the server at `http://localhost:5001` is running. You can do this by navigating to `http://localhost:5001` in your web browser or using a tool like curl or Postman to send a request to the server.

2. Check the server's code to ensure that the `/proxy/blocks` endpoint exists and is correctly configured.

3. If you're running the server and the React app on the same machine, there shouldn't be any network issues. However, if they're on different machines, you'll need to ensure that the network allows traffic between them.

4. If the server and endpoint are correctly set up and running, and there are no network issues, the problem could be with the request itself. Check the request headers and body to ensure they're correctly formatted.

If you're still having trouble after trying these steps, please provide more information about your setup and I'll be happy to help further.

Great, now that we have the server.js file, we can check if the endpoint `/proxy/blocks` is correctly set up. 

Here is the content of your server.js file:

```javascript
const express = require('express');
const cors = require('cors');
const proxy = require('./src/proxy');

const app = express();

// Enable CORS for all routes
app.use(cors());

app.use('/proxy/blocks', proxy);

app.get('/', (req, res) => {
  res.send('Server is running. Use /proxy for the proxy service.');
});

app.listen(5001, () => {
  console.log('Server is running on http://localhost:5001');
});
```

> btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js  
> Add these files to the chat? y  

Great, now that we have the `proxy.js` file, we can check how the proxy is set up. 

Here is the content of your `proxy.js` file:

```javascript
const axios = require('axios');

module.exports = function(req, res) {
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
    .then(response => {
      if (response.status === 200) {
        if (Array.isArray(response.data)) {
          res.json(response.data);
        } else {
          res.json([response.data]);
        }
      } else {
        console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', response.status);
        res.status(response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    })
    .catch(error => {
      console.error('Error fetching data from blockchain.com API:', error);
      if (error.response) {
        res.status(error.response.status).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      } else {
        res.status(500).json({ error: 'Error fetching data from blockchain.com API' });
      }
    });
};
```

From the code, it seems like the proxy is set up to make a GET request to `https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json` and return the response. 

The error message you're seeing indicates that this request is returning a 404 status code, which means the requested resource could not be found. 

This could be due to several reasons:

1. The URL `https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json` is incorrect or the resource does not exist.
2. There's a network issue preventing the request from reaching the `blockchain.info` server.

Here are some steps you can take to troubleshoot this issue:

1. Ensure that the URL `https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json` is correct and the resource exists. You can do this by navigating to the URL in your web browser or using a tool like curl or Postman to send a request to the URL.

2. If you're still having trouble after trying these steps, please provide more information about your setup and I'll be happy to help further.

Thank you for providing the API documentation. It seems that the endpoint you are trying to hit (`https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json`) does not exist according to the documentation. 

The correct endpoint to get the latest block data is `https://blockchain.info/latestblock`.

So, you need to modify the `proxy.js` file to use the correct endpoint.

Here is the *edit block* for the change:

```javascript
btc-rpc-explorer-react/src/proxy.js
<<<<<<< HEAD
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/blocks?format=json')
=======
  axios.get('https://blockchain.info/latestblock')
>>>>>>> updated
```

This change should fix the 404 error you are seeing. After making this change, try running your application again and see if the error is resolved.

```markdown
# Getting Started with Create React App

This project was bootstrapped with [Create React App](https://github.com/facebook/create-react-app).

## Available Scripts

In the project directory, you can run:

### `npm start`

Runs the app in the development mode.\
Open [http://localhost:3000](http://localhost:3000) to view it in your browser.

The page will reload when you make changes.\
You may also see any lint errors in the console.

### `npm test`

Launches the test runner in the interactive watch mode.\
See the section about [running tests](https://facebook.github.io/create-react-app/docs/running-tests) for more information.

### `npm run build`

Builds the app for production to the `build` folder.\
It correctly bundles React in production mode and optimizes the build for the best performance.

The build is minified and the filenames include the hashes.\
Your app is ready to be deployed!

See the section about [deployment](https://facebook.github.io/create-react-app/docs/deployment) for more information.

### `npm run eject`

**Note: this is a one-way operation. Once you `eject`, you can't go back!**

If you aren't satisfied with the build tool and configuration choices, you can `eject` at any time. This command will remove the single build dependency from your project.

Instead, it will copy all the configuration files and the transitive dependencies (webpack, Babel, ESLint, etc) right into your project so you have full control over them. All of the commands except `eject` will still work, but they will point to the copied scripts so you can tweak them. At this point you're on your own.

You don't have to ever use `eject`. The curated feature set is suitable for small and middle deployments, and you shouldn't feel obligated to use this feature. However we understand that this tool wouldn't be useful if you couldn't customize it when you are ready for it.

## Learn More

You can learn more in the [Create React App documentation](https://facebook.github.io/create-react-app/docs/getting-started).

To learn React, check out the [React documentation](https://reactjs.org/).
```
